    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="966c1bf8df2a4122822eaa636da25c16">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '83ffd014-e60c-457a-a5ba-0737bb9692a2'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d27298326" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) stands out as the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), and a precise adjustment of the pore structure is the key to achieving high plateau-capacity. In this work, composite hard carbon is developed by integrating graphitic carbon with biomass waste (banana peel)-derived activated carbon (AC). In this design, N-doped pseudographite layer is stacked at the entrance of open pores, forming a long-range graphitic layer without excessive graphitization. As a result, the surface area of AC is decreased by 170 times down to less than 10&nbsp;m<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, and the corresponding open pores are in situ converted into closed pores. In an optimized electrolyte solvation structure, the obtained HC anode achieves the reversible sodium-storage capacity up to 524 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. In particular, a large portion of the capacity (490 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) lies below the plateau of 0.25&nbsp;V, which originates from the pore-filling mechanism as revealed by in situ Raman. This study provides a straightforward method to modulate the pore structure of carbon materials, and an energy-efficient (900 °C) synthesis for HC compared to traditional high-temperature routes (e.g., ≈1300–2000 °C).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410326-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410326-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>It is increasingly recognized that harnessing renewable energy sources, reshaping the energy infrastructure, and gradually phasing out traditional fossil fuels are vital steps toward sustainability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0001_R_d27298154e324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0002_R_d27298154e324" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Acta Phys. Chim. Sin.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Electrochemical energy storage, distinguished by high-energy density, high-power capability, and environmental sustainability, has emerged as a pivotal component within the energy storage domain.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0003_R_d27298154e329" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Mater. Today Energy.">3</a>-<a href="#adma202410326-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0006_R_d27298154e329" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Mater. Today Energy.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Compared to lithium, sodium resources are abundant and the overall cost of sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is about 12% lower than Li-ion batteries due to the elimination of copper foil as the current collector.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0007_R_d27298154e334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0008_R_d27298154e334" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Compared to the cathode materials (e.g., polyanionic compounds, Prussian blues, layered oxides), the development of anode materials is relatively slow. The commercial graphite anode in Li-ion batteries is not suitable for sodium storage due to the relatively large size of sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0009_R_d27298154e342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - RSC Adv.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hard carbon (HC), a form of nongraphitized carbon characterized by randomly oriented carbon layers and nanodomains with intricate and diverse structures as well as a high proportion of closed nanopores, has been regarded as the most suitable anode material for commercial SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0010_R_d27298154e347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Storage Mater.">10</a>-<a href="#adma202410326-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0012_R_d27298154e347" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Mater. Today.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Until now, the sodium storage mechanism of hard carbons is still under debate. Different models have been proposed, such as intercalation-filling, adsorption–insertion, adsorption–insertion-filling, and adsorption-filling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0008_R_d27298154e352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0013_R_d27298154e352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#adma202410326-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0015_R_d27298154e352" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nat. Energy.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recent research has highlighted the significance of closed pores as effective active sites to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> ions,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0016_R_d27298154e360" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Natl. Sci. Rev.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which can contribute to the plateau capacity. In order to create abundant closed nanopores in HCs, various activation agents (such as Mn<sup>2+</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0017_R_d27298154e368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MgO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0018_R_d27298154e373" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> KOH,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0019_R_d27298154e378" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Power Sources.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> CO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0020_R_d27298154e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ZnO,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0021_R_d27298154e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Ni<sup>2+</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0022_R_d27298154e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ethanol<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0023_R_d27298154e404" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Energy Lett.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) have been introduced to etch different precursors (e.g., cellulose, carbohydrates, wood, starch, and resins) during the carbonation process. The plateau capacity has been considerably improved in these reports. In addition, some biomass (such as corn cobs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0024_R_d27298154e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> silk fibroin<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0025_R_d27298154e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) can be converted into topological HC through an extremely high-temperature annealing process (over 2000 °C) and all-plateau sodium insertion has been achieved with a Na-storage capacity of about 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>Despite these progress, cost-effective mass production of high-performance hard carbon still faces several challenges. First, the reversible Na-storage capacity of current hard carbons is highly sensitive to the selection of precursors. For example, carbohydrates (e.g., glucose,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0026_R_d27298154e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> starch,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0027_R_d27298154e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> saccharose<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0028_R_d27298154e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>)-derived hard carbons can easily achieve high specific capacity, but the high cost (&gt;5000 Chinese Yuan/ton) of raw materials and low carbon yield (less 10%) are unfavorable for industrial adoption. Other low-cost precursors (e.g., anthracite<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0029_R_d27298154e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>) correspond to poor capacity. Second, while introduction of activation agents can boost the low-potential plateau capacity, removing them is complicated and time-consuming, and even may result in morphology collapse and low tap density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0030_R_d27298154e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Carbon.">30</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0029_R_d27298154e446" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The last and most important, the typical high-temperature carbonation process (≈1300–2000 °C) together with a low ramping rate (e.g., 2 °C min<sup>−1</sup>) inevitably consume high energy for Kiloton production. Hence, it is technologically important to explore effective and sustainable approach to fabricate hard carbons by enriching the portion of closed pores as effective active sites for sodium-ion storage.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we have synthesized hard carbon through chemical activation of biomass waste (here banana peel) and subsequent catalytic decomposition of industrial-grade solvents (such as acetonitrile, benzene). The pyrolyzed pseudographitic layer gradually accumulates at the pore entrance of activated carbon (AC), which helps sieve out the solvated sodium ions to limit the electrolyte decomposition. As a result, the closed pore volume of the optimal HC material becomes ten times that of original sample without depositing graphitic layers. Because of this tailored HC, together with optimized electrolyte, we have pushed the reversible plateau-capacity below 0.25&nbsp;V up to 490 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Considering the low annealing temperature (900 °C) and the fast heat rate (10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>), the electricity consumption of our synthesis is estimated to be only 1/3 lower than that for traditional hard carbons. This study also showcases the “turning trash into treasure” practice, imposing potential for scalable energy storage application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410326-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410326-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410326-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410326-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Design and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>We employed a chemical vapor deposition (CVD) method to transform open pores into closed ones (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410326-fig-0001">1a</a>). For a case study, banana peel as the typical biomass waste is selected as the precursor to prepare activated carbon through precarbonization and subsequent alkali activation at 900 °C. The AC serves as the catalytic substrate to boost the uniform growth of graphitic carbon (GC) in the CVD process. Specifically, acetonitrile (CH<sub>3</sub>CN) is selected as the carbon resource of GC because it contains nitrogen. The as-formed N-doping sites can enhance the binding energy between GC and AC, and also expand the layer spacing to elevate the Na<sup>+</sup>-storage performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0031_R_d27298154e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Energy Chem.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the acetonitrile molecule traverses the surface of AC, it tends to adsorb onto the walls of the slit pores within the AC due to the intermolecular van der Waals forces between the π orbital on the carbon walls of AC and the electronic cloud in the −C≡N group of acetonitrile.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0032_R_d27298154e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Nanosci.">32</a>-<a href="#adma202410326-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0035_R_d27298154e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the adsorbed CH<i><sub>x</sub></i> and N species undergoes thermal pyrolysis, thus forming a flat graphitic layer. The growth on the pore wall continues until the pore size becomes smaller than the size of the acetonitrile molecule (0.45&nbsp;nm), which will prevent further entry of acetonitrile molecules into nanopores of AC.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0036_R_d27298154e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Catal. Today.">36</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0037_R_d27298154e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Carbon.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the pore repairing process, the deposited GC layer will impede additional acetonitrile gas to diffuse deep into the open pores. This finally results in the creation of closed micropores within the bulk of the carbon materials. It is noted that our designed synthesis strategy is not limited to banana peel, but can be extended to any carbon-containing precursors for the commercial AC-production line and subsequent GC deposition. For comparison, banana peel is directly annealed at 1300 °C to obtain traditional HC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410326-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3193edc6-3583-47d1-b498-b74b0c150fb4/adma202410326-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3193edc6-3583-47d1-b498-b74b0c150fb4/adma202410326-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1e487cf-f643-4a00-a965-ee0bd3823cb8/adma202410326-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3193edc6-3583-47d1-b498-b74b0c150fb4/adma202410326-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410326-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410326" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis and structural features. a) Schematic diagram for synthesizing RC-X (X represents the CVD deposition time). TEM images of samples with different deposition times (insets are magnified views). b) AC. c) RC-30. d) RC-90. e) RC-240. f) XRD patterns. g) Variations in <i>d</i><sub>002</sub>, <i>R</i>, <i>L</i><sub>a</sub>, and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> derived from XRD calculations, and h) Raman spectra.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The effect of the deposition condition (e.g., time, temperature, carbon resource) on microstructures of AC is systematically examined. These CVD-repairing carbon materials are denoted as “RC-X” (X represents the deposition time). Weight increases with deposition time (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). The RC samples show consistently smooth surfaces, indicating acetonitrile decomposition within the pores rather than mere surface stacking on AC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the AC exhibits a highly disordered structure characterized by disorganized lattice stripes and a large layer spacing of 0.442&nbsp;nm in high-resolution transmission electron microscopy images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0001">1b–e</a>). As the deposition time increases, the lattice stripes tend to regularize and grow along the length and thickness directions. The layer spacing of RC-90 diminishes to 0.383&nbsp;nm, with noteworthy pseudographite layers deposited at 0.376&nbsp;nm spacing. The formation of these pseudographite layers not only leads to reduced O content and fewer defect sites, which are advantageous for initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE), but also enables the intercalation and deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Elemental mapping of RC-90 reveals the uniform distribution of C, O, and N elements within the particles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). However, after a longer deposition time of 240&nbsp;min, the lattice stripes become highly graphitized, and the carbon layer formed by acetonitrile pyrolysis becomes excessively thick with a layer spacing of 0.352&nbsp;nm, which will hinder Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation.</p>
            
            <p>The carbon microcrystals of the RC samples were further characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD) and Raman spectroscopy. The XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0001">1f</a>) shows two broad peaks at nearly 23° and 43°, which are related to the (002) and (100) lattice planes of RC-X. As the deposition time increases, the peak position of (002) is shifted to the right and both broad peaks become progressively sharper. The structural information of the material can be calculated based on the two peaks in the XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0001">1g</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). It can be noticed that as the deposition time increases, the value of <i>d</i> decreases, while <i>R</i>, <i>L</i><sub>a</sub>, and <i>L</i><sub>c</sub> all increase. This indicates a reduction in interlayer distance, accompanied by an increase in the degree of carbon ordering, the average width of the graphite crystals along the <i>a</i>-axis, and the stack thickness along the <i>c</i>-axis. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0001">1h</a> presents the corresponding Raman spectra, which reveal the presence of two typical characteristic peaks, the D-band at around 1340 cm<sup>−1</sup> and G-band at around 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>. The typical intensity ratio of D to G peak (<i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub>) decreases from 1.037 (RC-30) to 0.979 (RC-240). This trend implies the increased degree of graphitization, which is consistent with the TEM and XRD results. The relatively high <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio in RC-30 may be due to nitrogen doping and defect dominance, as a high <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value (1.024) is also obtained in acetonitrile-pyrolyzed N-doping carbon (denoted as “AN-C”) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). X-ray photoemission spectroscopy (XPS) reveals that the increase in pyridinic-N and pyrrolic-N leads to additional defects in the graphene layers, as well as the transformation from C<i>─</i>O to C═O as the deposition time increases, which is beneficial for achieving high-performance Na-storage (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0038_R_d27298154e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Commun.">38</a>-<a href="#adma202410326-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0040_R_d27298154e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Mater. Today Energy.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>Thermogravimetry and differential thermogravimetry (TG-DTG) were used to determine the thermal stability of as-prepared AC and RC-90 (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2a</a>). In both TG curves, the only significant weight loss corresponds to carbon combustion. Both samples show no mass residual, indicating their high purity without ash content. In addition, the slightly higher burning temperature for RC-90 (579 °C) than AC (526 °C) is mainly caused by high thermal stability of the pseudographite layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0041_R_d27298154e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2b</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a>, Supporting Information) show that the HC directly annealed at 1300 °C and AC obtained by chemical activation exhibits a specific surface area of 540.63 and 1692.44&nbsp;m<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. After growing graphitic layers on AC, the specific surface areas are all reduced for all the RC samples (see Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2b</a>), suggesting a gradual blockage of open pores by pyrolytic acetonitrile. The pore size distributions obtained using the nonlocal density functional theory (NLDFT) method indicate that the original micropores in AC gradually become inaccessible by N<sub>2</sub> molecules (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2c</a>). Therefore, we performed CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2d</a>) to explore the distribution of ultra-micropores with a diameter less than 1&nbsp;nm. It should be noted that N or O functional groups contribute to the CO<sub>2</sub> sorption in the lower pressure ranges, which interrupts the micropore analysis. A large number of micropores exist in RC-90 with pore size ranging between 0.4–0.6&nbsp;nm and pore volume of 0.0026&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. These “super-micropores” will function as ionic sieves, reducing the diffusion of solvated Na<sup>+</sup> while only permitting naked Na<sup>+</sup> to enter the pores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0042_R_d27298154e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Adv. Energy Mater.">42</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0043_R_d27298154e683" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Rare Met.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hence, they may contribute to adsorption capacity but not to the low-plateau capacity. This phenomenon leads to a decreased interfacial contact between the electrolyte and the inner pores without compromising diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0044_R_d27298154e688" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano-Micro Lett.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410326-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aac0e213-bc56-45e8-b7e6-bfc6e7d390cd/adma202410326-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/aac0e213-bc56-45e8-b7e6-bfc6e7d390cd/adma202410326-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6839d225-8384-402a-ade3-945a0c12d153/adma202410326-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aac0e213-bc56-45e8-b7e6-bfc6e7d390cd/adma202410326-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410326-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410326" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Structural characterizations of AC and RC-X. a) TG and DTG curves, b) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms. c) Pore size distribution of selected samples. d) CO<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption curves and microporous pore size distributions of RC-90. e) SAXS curves, and f) Closed pore size and pore volume of AC and RC-90. g) A summary of structural features of RC-X with different deposition times.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Small-angle X-ray scattering (SAXS) is employed for analyzing the characteristics of closed pores that cannot be detected by N<sub>2</sub> and CO<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0045_R_d27298154e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">45</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0046_R_d27298154e724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Chem. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2e</a>, the SAXS curves of AC and RC-90 before 0.1 Å<sup>−1</sup> reflect the structural features on a larger scale, and the intensity changes after 0.1 Å<sup>−1</sup> show a “plateau” and then decrease, which can be attributed to the presence of closed pores. In this region, the scattering intensity of RC-90 is obviously stronger than that of AC, indicating that the closed pores in RC-90 have largely increased. The average closed pore aperture can be derived by fitting the SAXS curve (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The closed pore radius of RC-90 (1.342&nbsp;nm) is higher than that of AC (0.912&nbsp;nm). These closed pores provide transport channels for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion to increase the plateau capacity and also to inhibit electrolyte contact with the internal carbon surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0044_R_d27298154e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Nano-Micro Lett.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> True density refers to the density after removal of closed pores and intergranular voids, and graphite with no closed pores has a true density of 2.26&nbsp;g cm<sup>−3</sup>. Calculated from <i>V</i><sub>Closed Pore</sub> =  1/ρ<sub>true</sub> − 1/2.26,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0047_R_d27298154e755" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Adv. Energy Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the closed pore volume of RC-90 is 0.34&nbsp;816&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2f</a>; and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), which is 10 times that of AC (0.0313&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>). This demonstrates that open pores in AC become more closed after the deposition treatment. Based on the above characterization results, structural variation (interlayer distance, stacking layers, oxygen content, closed pores) of various RC samples is illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0002">2g</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410326-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410326-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium Storage Performance</h3>
            
            <p>Half-cell tests are first conducted at a current density of 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> using commercial 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in 1,2-dimethoxyethane (DME, denoted as “1M-ND”) as the electrolyte to assess the electrochemical performance of the RC-X anodes. In the galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410326-fig-0003">3a</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8b</a>, Supporting Information), the AC sample shows only slope-voltage profiles due to its abundance of open pores. In contrast, the charge–discharge plateau is observed after CVD deposition. This is because open pores in AC are gradually converted to closed pores, and the RC-X samples show similar discharge–charge curves to traditional hard carbon, namely the sloping region and the plateau region. The specific surface area of the samples obtained by treating AC only at 900 °C for 90&nbsp;min did not change significantly and there was no plateau capacity (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the graphite layer formed by pyrolysis of acetonitrile blocked the pore entrance rather than the high temperature treatment. The plateau capacity of the RC-X samples increases with increasing deposition time, which is attributed to the formation of more closed pores. Among them, RC-90 has the highest reversible capacity of 465.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, of which the plateau capacity is about 415.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with a plateau fraction of about 90%, and the ICE of 86.81%. It should be noted that the reversible capacity, the plateau capacity, and the ICE value decrease for the deposition time greater than 90&nbsp;min. This is probably due to the reduction of internal closed pores in the material, and the highly graphitized structure with the layer spacing of less than 0.37&nbsp;nm are unfavorable for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410326-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/bd13dc3c-8c05-4a1e-978e-719eee44df62/adma202410326-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/bd13dc3c-8c05-4a1e-978e-719eee44df62/adma202410326-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f3c5e917-f2df-45bf-a6db-835cb16bd6e3/adma202410326-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/bd13dc3c-8c05-4a1e-978e-719eee44df62/adma202410326-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410326-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410326" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium ion storage performance of RC anodes. a) GCD curves at various deposition times at the deposition temperature of 900 °C. b) GCD curves at different deposition temperatures and different carbon sources at a deposition time of 90&nbsp;min. c) Comparison of plateau capacity and ICE of all samples (original data are in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). d) Cycling stability of RC-90 at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Rate capability of RC-90.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The effects of deposition temperature, carbon source, and substrate carbon material on the degree of pore repairing are also investigated. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0003">3b</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), the samples obtained by deposition at 750 °C have a high percentage of slope capacity due to less graphitization and more defects; And the samples obtained by deposition at 1050 °C exhibit a correspondingly lower specific capacity due to excessive graphitization as well as reduced internal closed pores. Further, replacing the carbon source with benzene ends up with noticeably lower reversible capacity and plateau capacity (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0003">3b</a>). This outcome could be attributed to the high stability of the benzene ring, as opposed to the acetonitrile molecule. Acetonitrile is more susceptible to cleavage, leading to the formation of smaller molecules and the generation of a regular N-doping pseudographite layer on the pore wall. Comparing the GCD curves of carbon electrodes obtained from benzene and pyridine sources, it is evident that N-doping can reduce the voltage in the sloping region (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Hence, within the voltage range of 0.25–1&nbsp;V, N-doping can accelerate the adsorption and intercalation due to defects. All above results are summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0003">3c</a>. When we extended this approach to materials with different pore sizes (such as Ketjen Black, Super P), it is found that only when the micropores dominate with appropriate pore sizes, more closed pores suitable for sodium storage will be formed during the repairing process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>Although the RC-90 sample exhibits the highest reversible specific capacity and enhanced ICE, its cycling performance in the conventional 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>-DME electrolyte is unsatisfactory, with a capacity retention of only 34.77% after 100 cycles at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0003">3d</a>). The capacity diminishes to less than 100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 300&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0003">3e</a>). Similar to RC-90, other RC-X samples show rapid capacity loss (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). Consequently, we turn to optimize the electrolyte to enhance both cycling stability and high-rate performance of the RC-90 sample.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410326-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410326-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrolyte Optimization</h3>
            
            <p>In recent years, electrolytes with high salt concentrations can improve the cycle stability of anode materials. Here, we intend to elevate the molar concentration of NaPF<sub>6</sub> to enhance the Na-storage stability of the RC-90 anode. To retain the liquid state without crystallization and precipitation even at −10 °C, we added diluent 1,3-dioxolane (DOL)34(<i>V</i><sub>DME</sub>: <i>V</i><sub>DOL</sub> = 10:1) to ensure complete dissolution of 2&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME/DOL, resulting in the formulation of the 2M-NDD electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> in the Supporting Information). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4a</a> presents the solvation model for various electrolyte concentrations. In the 1M-ND scenario, balance exists between the number of negative ions and solvent, resulting in good ion conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0048_R_d27298154e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">48</a>-<a href="#adma202410326-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0051_R_d27298154e914" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Energy Environ. Sci.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, free anions and solvent tend to decompose near the solid electrolyte interface (SEI). With an increase in electrolyte concentration, the solvation structure of the Na<sup>+</sup> ions changes, leading to a higher tendency of ion pairs formation and aggregation of anions due to insufficient number of solvent molecules. This will reduce the solvent decomposition near the SEI, thereby enhancing the interface stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0052_R_d27298154e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Sci.">52</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0053_R_d27298154e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Energy Storage Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, in high concentration electrolytes, the absence of a solvent cointercalation layer accelerates the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion rate, but adversely the elevated salt content will increase electrolyte viscosity and limit the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0054_R_d27298154e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Phys. Chem. B.">54</a>-<a href="#adma202410326-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0056_R_d27298154e932" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Conversely, the addition of diluent DOL preserves the local solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> and reduces viscosity, thereby promoting the cycling stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0052_R_d27298154e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Sci.">52</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0057_R_d27298154e939" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Nat. Energy.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410326-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/89696a0e-bf26-4d95-9258-1cc8dee016c4/adma202410326-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/89696a0e-bf26-4d95-9258-1cc8dee016c4/adma202410326-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fae11589-25e2-443d-9d1d-645c881e9c17/adma202410326-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/89696a0e-bf26-4d95-9258-1cc8dee016c4/adma202410326-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410326-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410326" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Optimization of electrolyte solvation structure. a) Brief modeling of solvated structures for electrolytes with different concentrations. b–d) GCD curves, cyclic stability at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and rate performance of RC-90 in 2M-NDD electrolyte. e) Sodium-ion diffusion coefficients at sodiation and desodiation process estimated from GITT curves in 1M-ND and 2M-NDD electrolytes. f) EIS analysis of RC-90 electrodes after the discharge process in 1M-ND and 2M-NDD electrolytes. Comparison of SEI after 20 cycles in 1M-ND and 2M-NDD electrolytes: g) TEM image, h) F 1s spectra.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The solvation structure of NaPF<sub>6</sub> in DME (or DME/DOL) was characterized by Raman spectra (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The weakly Na<sup>+</sup>-coordinating DOL only disperses outside the primary solvent shell as a diluent, and the introduction of DOL leads to a predominance of Na<sup>+</sup>-coordinated DME molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0058_R_d27298154e977" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the peak at ≈740 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to the P<i>─</i>F bond, gradually increases with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> concentration, suggesting that PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> does not enter the Na<sup>+</sup> solvation shell.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0059_R_d27298154e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">59</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0060_R_d27298154e995" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nat. Commun.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In summary, we have achieved an almost fully solvated DME, coordinated to Na<sup>+</sup> as the inner solvation sheath.</p>
            
            <p>Comparing the GCD curves in different electrolytes (1M-ND, 2M-ND, 2M-NDD), similar intercalation properties are observed in the RC-90 half-cells. However, the RC-90 is still unstable when the molar concentration of NaPF<sub>6</sub> is increased to 2&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> (2M-ND), with up to 66.67% capacity decay for only 50 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), which may be due to the high viscosity of the electrolyte (low ion mobility). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4b</a>, a high specific capacity of 524 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is measured for RC-90 in the 2M-NDD electrolyte, corresponding to a high plateau capacity of up to 490 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (below 0.25&nbsp;V). The GCD curves in the 2M-NDD electrolyte overlap completely, except for the first cycle due to the formation of SEI. In comparison, the GCD curves in the 1M-ND electrolyte do not overlap well. This is an indication that RC-90 has better cycle stability in 2M-NDD electrolyte. Compared to 1M-ND, 2M-NDD shows better stability with 93.37% capacity retention after 180 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4c</a>). The capacity retains over 500 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 50 cycles at 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). As for rate performance, the reversible capacity recovers to 515.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> when the current density is back to 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4d</a>). Even at a large current of 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity of RC-90 is still stable after 300 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), demonstrating good compatibility between the 2M-NDD electrolyte and the ultrahigh-capacity RC-90 anode. Herein, 2&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> is proven the minimum concentration that can stabilize RC-90 while improving its rate performance (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, our RC-90 exhibits superior sodium storage performance compared to previously reported hard carbon electrodes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), with a plateau capacity exceeding 90%.</p>
            
            <p>Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and interfacial chemistry analysis using SEM, TEM, and XPS measurements are further conducted to compare Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics in two different electrolytes. Detailed analysis is presented in the Supporting Information (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22–S28</a>, Supporting Information) and we only discuss main conclusion here. The higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients in the 2M-NDD electrolyte than in the 1M-ND electrolyte during both during charging and discharging suggest that the Na<sup>+</sup>-diffusion kinetics in the 2M-NDD electrolyte are effectively accelerated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4e</a>). The resistances originated from the SEI film and charge transfer in 2M-NDD electrolyte are both lower than those in 1M-ND electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4f</a>; and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting information). The SEI film formed in the 1M-ND electrolyte is between 9.25 and 16.57&nbsp;nm with uneven thickness, while the SEI in the 2M-NDD electrolyte is much thinner (3.59–5.66&nbsp;nm) and more uniform (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4g</a>). The fine XPS spectra of F 1s were analyzed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0004">4h</a>). The peak at 289.5&nbsp;eV (<i>─</i>CF<sub>X</sub><i>─</i>) and the peaks at 683.6&nbsp;eV (NaF), 687.0&nbsp;eV (F<i>─</i>P), and 689.5&nbsp;eV (C<i>─</i>F<sub>X</sub>) in the F 1s spectrum are related to the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>. The peak area of NaF, which is a coarse measure of the inorganic content in the SEI layer,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0055_R_d27298154e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Power Sources.">55</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0061_R_d27298154e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Sci. Bull.">61</a>, <a href="#adma202410326-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0062_R_d27298154e1098" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nat. Energy.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> is larger in the 2M-NDD than in the 1M-ND, indicating that the SEI layer formed by 2M-NDD contains more inorganic components. The inorganic components in the SEI usually have a lower Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion activation energy (NaF≈100–150 MeV), which facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> migration at the electrode–electrolyte interface and prevents continuous electrolyte decomposition. In conclusion, the thinner, homogeneous and NaF-rich SEI generated in the 2M-NDD electrolyte favors the cycling stability of RC-90.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202410326-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202410326-sec-0060-title">2.4 Sodium Storage Mechanism and Na-Ion Full Cells</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the sodium storage mechanism of RC-90 in the plateau region, we conducted charge–discharge tests at various voltages, followed by the removal of the electrode sheet and immersion in a prepared ethanol/phenolphthalein solution. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates the solution displaying varying degrees of purple-red color at voltages below 0.1&nbsp;V, indicating that Na clusters stored in closed pores react with ethanol to generate H<sub>2</sub> and CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>ONa. The alkaline components in the resulting products induce a change in the color of phenolphthalein. Furthermore, the color of the solution intensifies as the voltage gradually decreases to 0.01&nbsp;V, suggesting a greater storage of Na clusters in the closed pores. Additionally, the color is lightened as the voltage increases, confirming the reversibility of Na cluster formation during the sodiation/desodiation process. This phenomenon is not observed in AC, implying the closed-hole sodium filling mechanism in RC-90. As depicted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting information), a noticeable shift to a lower angle of the XRD diffraction peak at 24° and the appearance of the characteristic peak of sodium metal are observed when the discharge voltage drops below 0.1&nbsp;V. These features indicate the insertion of sodium into the carbon layers and the filling of carbon nanopores. During the desodiation process, the (002) peaks return to higher angles, and the sodium metal peak disappears, suggesting a contraction of the interlayer space and the detachment of Na clusters from the closed pores.</p>
            
            <p>To delve deep into the electrochemical reaction mechanism and structural evolution of the RC-90 electrode, we conducted ex situ XRD and in situ Raman analyses. The in situ Raman spectra of the RC-90 in 2M-NDD are illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410326-fig-0005">5a–c</a>. The D-band peak gradually broadens and decreases throughout the discharging process from 1.7 to 0.5&nbsp;V, while the peak position remains unchanged, indicating that Na<sup>+</sup> in this stage are primarily controlled by adsorption. As the discharge continues to the plateau voltage of 0.5–0.01&nbsp;V, the G-band shifts to a lower frequency of 1550 cm<sup>−1</sup>, and the D-band nearly disappears, suggesting the occupation of defect sites on the carbon surface. The shift in the G-peak position results from electron doping due to the gradual insertion and homogeneous diffusion of sodium into each layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410326-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410326-bib-0024_R_d27298154e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These results support the proposal that the storage of Na<sup>+</sup> relies on adsorption and insertion in pseudographite interlayer in the slope region, intercalation and filling of closed pores in the plateau region (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0005">5d</a>). During the charging process, the changes in peak position and intensity are completely opposite, indicating the good reversibility of sodium storage in RC-90.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202410326-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/24aa51da-db03-4849-921d-87dbabbd19bc/adma202410326-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/24aa51da-db03-4849-921d-87dbabbd19bc/adma202410326-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5924491d-8cf4-4ac8-8d03-c386302b8159/adma202410326-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/24aa51da-db03-4849-921d-87dbabbd19bc/adma202410326-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410326-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410326" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage mechanism and full SIB devices. a) In situ Raman spectra with time-potential of the RC-90 electrode during the first charge/discharge cycle. b,c) In situ Raman spectra of RC-90 during the discharge/charge process. d) Schematic diagram of the sodium storage stages of RC-90. e) GCD curves of NVP under 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. f) Charge/discharge curves of RC-90//NVP full cell at various current densities. g) Cycling performance of RC-90//NVP full cell at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Finally, a sodium-ion full cell was assembled using a RC-90 anode coupled with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode and the 2M-NDD electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a>, Supporting information). The charge–discharge curves of NVP cathode present characteristic plateau around 3. 38&nbsp;V (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0005">5e</a>). The assembled full cell delivers a high capacity of 105.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 25&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and 69 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at an increased current density of 800&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> with clear plateau (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0005">5f</a>). The cell retains 92% of its initial capacity after 500 cycles at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410326-fig-0005">5g</a>). Moreover, our RC-90//NVP SIB can deliver an energy density of 329.2&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 78&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> and maintain 190.5&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> at 2220&nbsp;W kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the mass of active materials in both electrodes). In short, we have shown that RC-90 is a high-performance anode material for practical SIBs.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410326-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410326-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>We have developed a new type of high-capacity hard carbon anode by integrating biomass-derived activated carbon with N-doped pseudographitic layers. This integration can tune the micropore size and volume and convert open pores to closed micropores. Upon the optimization of deposition parameter and electrolyte solvation structure, the hard carbon anode delivers a specific capacity of 524&nbsp;m Ah g<sup>−1</sup>, with a corresponding plateau capacity as high as 490&nbsp;m Ah g<sup>−1</sup>, and a capacity retention of over 96.15% after 150 cycles. It is found that, 1) insufficient deposition time prevents open pores from evolving into closed pores suitable for sodium storage, while excessive deposition time will lead to complete pore blockage and over-graphitization, subsequently impeding sodium ion channels; 2) The addition of diluent preserves the local solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> and forms the thin, homogeneous and NaF-rich SEI generated in the 2M-NDD electrolyte, thus enhancing the cycling and rate performance of RC-90. The ultrahigh plateau-capacity sodium storage in this integrated hard carbon anode is attributed to the Na cluster formation within closed pores. This synthesis strategy is generic and can be extended to many carbon precursors for the commercial production of hard carbons toward grid-scale energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

                  <li data-bib-id="adma202410326-bib-0004"><span class="bullet">4</span><span class="author">Z.-F. Mao</span>, <span class="author">X.-J. Shi</span>, <span class="author">T.-Q.
